Abstract
In this thesis focus is on the capacity calculations regarding rotational L-
block tearing in gusset plates in bolted connections. A bolted connection
can be complicated and time-consuming to calculate, why it is sought to do
after simple calculation methods. With the simpliп¬Ѓcation, of course, it must
be ensured that the calculation method also yield reliable results.
In Denmark, calculation rules are stated by the European standards, which
for bolted connections are covered by DS/EN 1993-1-8 [5]. By this standard,
however, it has been found that the capacity calculation regarding block
tearing, has become too simple, when concern is made on an eccentric load
of the connection. This results in unreliability in the capacity calculation,
which has been found to indicate much greater capacities than found by ex-
periments.
Initially, a literature study has been carried out, in which some of the avail-
able literature on the subject has been reviewed. In this context, it was made
clear that a lot of experiments has been performed and a great deal of work
has been made regarding concentric applied load, while eccentric loaded con-
nections hardly has been investigated.
This thesis deals with this subject, by trying to п¬Ѓnd new capacity formu-
las that to a greater extend demonstrate the capacity found by experiments.
The Experiments that are compared with are made by fellow students who,
in their thesis, have performed experiments on eccentrically loaded gusset
plates connections. In the experiments, one bolt layout is examined, but
with varying eccentricities.
The theory of plasticity is used to determine the stress distribution that
occurs in the block as it is torn out. In 2015 J. JВЁonsson made a proposal for
this distribution, in what is called Generalized Block Failure [15]. By the use
of the theory of plasticity, simple and permissible equilibrium can be estab-
v
==PAGE:6==
lished. This is done both regarding normal force, shear force and moment,
where several diп¬Ђerent distributions are investigated. For the combination
of the stresses that arise from the inп¬‚uence of these forces, yield criteria are
established, whereby an actual capacity can be described.
The yield criterion given by von Mises, which is usually used to determine
the capacity of steel structures, is investigated in more detail. At diп¬Ђerent
combinations of strains, it is attempted do derive a greater capacity by the
yield criterion. It is shown that with uni-axial strain, a greater capacity can
actually be found.
In the thesis it is also examined whether it is possible to predict the de-
formation behaviour of the block, by implementation of a new block tearing
component into the Component Method. The Component Method is based
on the theory of elasticity, but tries to predict behaviour after yield in the
block has developed. Therefore a number of assumptions are implemented
that together make the method and its results uncertain.
After comparing the impending theory and observations of the experiments
and their data, three approaches are implemented in the attempt to improve
the the capacity expression regarding rotational block tearing. It is seen that
great increase in the capacity formula is found.
Lastly, a п¬Ѓnal proposal is given on what can be implemented in the stan-
dard, thus ensuring that the capacity formula is both simple and reliable.

ResumВґe
I dette speciale fokuseres pЛљa beregningen af kapaciteten ved rotationel L-
blokforskydning i laskeplader i en boltesamling. En boltesamling kan vГ¦re
kompliceret og tidskrГ¦vende at beregne, derfor strГ¦bes der efter at dette kan
gГёres efter simple beregningsmetoder. Med simpliп¬Ѓceringen skal det natur-
ligvis sikres at beregningsmetoden ogsЛљa giver pЛљalidelige resultater.
I Danmark regnes der efter de EuropГ¦iske standarder, der for boltesamlin-
ger er omfattet af DS/EN 1993-1-8 [5]. Ved denne standard er der dog fun-
det at kapacitetsberegningen vedrГёrende blokforskydning er gjort lige simpel
nok, nЛљar der skal tages hГёjde for en excentrisk belastning af samlingen. Dette
medfГёrer upЛљalidelighed i kapacitetsberegningen, der er fundet at angive langt
stГёrre kapacitetet end det er tilfГ¦ldet ved forsГёg.
Indledningsvis er der lavet et litteraturstudie, hvor noget af den tilgГ¦nge-
lige litteratur pЛљa omrЛљadet er gennemgЛљaet. Det blev i denne forbindelse gjort
klart, at der generelt er foretaget mange forsГёg og gjort et stort arbejde i
frembringelsen af kapacitetsberegninger for en koncentrisk belastet samling,
mens den excentrisk belastede samling, nГ¦rmest ikke er undersГёgt.
Dette speciale omhandler denne problemstilling, idet der forsГёges at п¬Ѓnde
frem til nye kapacitets formler, der til en stГёrre grad efterviser den kapa-
citet der п¬Ѓndes ved forsГёg. ForsГёgende der sammenlignes med, er foretaget
af medstuderende, der i forbindelse med deres speciale har udfГёrt forsГёg pЛљa
excentrisk pЛљavirkede laskepladesamlinger. I forsГёgene undersГёges Вґen bolte op-
sГ¦tningen, men med varierende excentriciteter.
Plasticitetsteorien benyttes til at bestemme spГ¦ndingsfordelingen, der opstЛљar
i blokken, nЛљar denne rives ud. J. JВЁonsson opstillede i 2015 et forslag til den-
ne fordeling, i det der kaldes Generaliseret Blokudrivning [15]. Ved brug af
plasticitetsteorien, kan simple og tilladelige ligevГ¦gte opstilles. Dette gГёres
for bЛљade normalkraft, forskydningskraft og moment, idet п¬‚ere forskellige for-
vii
==PAGE:8==
delinger undersГёges. For kombinationen af de spГ¦ndinger der opstЛљar ved
pЛљavirknignen fra disse krГ¦fter, opstilles п¬‚ydekriterier, hvorved en egentlig
kapacitet kan beskrives.
Flydekriteriet givet af von Mises, der normaltvis benyttes til at п¬Ѓnde kapaci-
teten i stЛљalkonstruktioner, undersГёges nГ¦rmere. Ved forskellige kombinatio-
ner af tГёjninger, forsГёges det er udlede en stГёrre kapacitet ved п¬‚ydekriteriet.
Det ses at der ved en-akset tГёjning faktisk kan п¬Ѓndes stГёrre kapacitet.
I specialet undersГёges det ogsЛљa om det ved implementeringen af en ny blokfor-
skydnings komponent i Komponent Metoden, er muligt at forudsige opfГёrslen
af deformationerne. Komponent metoden baserer sig pЛљa elasticitetsteorien,
men forsГёger at sige noget om deformationerne efter п¬‚ydning i blokken. Der
implementeres derfor en rГ¦kke antagelser, der tilsammen gГёr metoden og
dens resultater usikre.
Efter sammenligning af den forestЛљaende teori og observationer ved ekspe-
rimenterne og deres data, implementers tre tiltag der skal forsГёge at forbedre
kapacitetsformlen for blokudrivningen. Det ses at store forbedringer i kapa-
citetsformlen kan п¬Ѓndes.
Til sidst gives et endeligt bud pЛљa, hvad der kan implementeres i standar-
den, sЛљaledes det sikres at kapacitetsformlen er bЛљade simpel og pЛљalidelig.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Bolted connections is a complex and rather time consuming part of the daily
work of the practising engineer. Therefore the essential need of simple and
reliable calculations is great. This need gathers and executes the Eurocode
[5], that acts as a standard for calculations done in the European region.
In this thesis concern is made on the phenomena of rotational block tear-
ing. Block tearing is a failure mechanism in which a block of material is
torn out in a combination of tension and shear failure. In the Eurocode con-
cern is also made on this phenomena, however, the reliability of the design
calculations is somewhat missing. For a concentric loaded connection, the
European standard, in a conservative sense, follows what have been observed
in the experimental п¬Ѓeld, whereas cases with connections subjected to an
eccentric load, is mistreated by the Eurocode.
Figure 1.1: Rotational block tearing failure
When a connection is subject to an eccentric applied load, a new com-
bination of forces arise, as a moment is induced by the eccentricity. This
1
==PAGE:16==
Introduction
introduction of moment is treated in a non-conservative manner by the Eu-
rocode by the application of a simple capacity reduction.
J. JВЁonsson questioned this mistreat and a suggestion for a better approx-
imation of the bearing capacity was made with the introduction of the Gen-
eralized Block Failure [15]. By this theory, the introduction of moment is
included by an assumption of how the forces might be distributed in the
yield lines around the block. Force equilibrium in the yield lines are obtained
through the theory of plasticity, that can be applied due to the yielding- and
ductile properties of the steel material.
Alongside the writing of this thesis, several experiments was performed on ec-
centric loaded bolted connections by student colleagues L.T. MГёlsted, A.E.A.
Nielsen and K.E. Czerniak. Variations in the eccentricity was included to se-
cure a wider base of experimental data. With the basis of this data and
observations during and after the experiments, the concern of this thesis is
made on the development of a design capacity expression for the rotational
block tearing phenomena, to yield a great resemblance with the actual ca-
pacity of the block. It is further tried to implement a new block tearing
component into the component method. The use of this component is to
predict the behaviour of the block.
Experiments was performed on both C- and L-block cutouts, however, the
main concern of this thesis is made on the L-cutout failure. The diп¬Ђerent
tests was divided into three: Type A, C-cutout with thin external plates,
Type B, L-cutout with thick internal plate and Type C, L-cutout with thin
external plates.
This thesis is divided into six chapters covering:
вЂў Chapter 1: Introduction
вЂў Chapter 2: Literature study - An investigation of the literature covering
the block tearing phenomena.
вЂў Chapter 3: Theory - The theoretical background needed to understand
and introduce new aspects into the capacity expressions is examined.
вЂў Chapter 4: Experiments - Data and observations from the experiments
is examined and compared to what is found in the chapter governing
theory.
2
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:17==
Introduction
вЂў Chapter 5: Theory development - A development of the theory based
on both experimental observations and what was studied in the theory
chapter. A comparison to the experimental data is further performed.
вЂў Chapter 6: Conclusion
Lastly the writer of this thesis performed tensile tests of dog-bones alongside
the writing, to determine the material properties of the plates used in the
connections. This is done to make better resemblance with the actual ca-
pacity of the connections, when a comparison is made with the theory. The
testing data is collected in a separate report [21].

Chapter 2
Literature study
For the State-of-the-art regarding rotational block tearing, the work done is
limited. In the following section, a literature study of some of the current
knowledge is reviewed.
The prior studies primary examines the capacity of block tearing with no
eccentricity. This behaviour is mainly seen in truss structures, where gus-
set plates are widely used as a connecting element.
In the latter studies
coped beams subjected to shear has been examined. In both examples, the
connections are subject to an acting force with no eccentricity.
2.1
Literature study
In the literature the number of articles concerning rotational block tearing
are few. On that note, the articles read mainly concentrates on the behaviour
of coped beams and gusset plates subjected to a concentric applied force.
In 1984 Hardash [22] examined the block-shear concept of steel gusset plates
loaded in pure tension. This was done by comparing theoretically results
with the results of 28 test subjects. By the study, Hardash concluded on the
fact that shear failure occurs on the gross shear area, while tension failure
was conducted on the net tension area. Further a correlation between con-
nection length and strength was seen, yielding an important parameter to be
incorporated in the gusset plate model. For shorter connections the ultimate
shear strength would be dominating, while for longer connections, the shear
yield strength would dominate the eп¬Ђective strength of the steel.
feff = (1 в€’cl)fy + cl fu
(2.1)

2.1 Literature study
Literature study
where cl = 0.95в€’0.047В·l в‰Ґ0, with l in inches, is the connection length factor.
Further it was seen that the tension area by good approximation could be
extended in a distance given by a line drawn from the п¬Ѓrst bolt row in an
angle of 30в—¦to the last bolt row, the so-called Whitmore eп¬Ђective width.
Figure 2.1: Whitmore eп¬Ђective width. Figure 3.1 in [22].
Cunningham et al. [2] studied the inп¬‚uence of several geometric- and material
parameters. In the study, they further investigated the inп¬‚uence of the load
acting with an eccentricity to the shear plane and one to the tension plane.
However, the eccentricity examined was almost not existing. Their primary
concern was the block shear in single angle tension members with only one
line of bolts. In their study several equation forms were considered, all with
the basic form similar to the one from the American standard AISC, namely a
factor diп¬Ђerent from unity on both the tension strength and shear strength.
By the objective to maintain a professional factor of the test-to-predicted
ratio mean within 2% of unity an optimization procedure on determining the
coeп¬ѓcients with focus on diп¬Ђerent parameters were employed. Their study
indicated that both aspect ratio and eccentricity to the shear plane may have
a signiп¬Ѓcant inп¬‚uence on the shear load contribution, but conversely does not
appear to have inп¬‚uence on the load contribution from the tension plane.
In the diп¬Ђerent equations, with the general form seen in equation (2.2),

A conclusion was as well made on the behaviour when failure occurred.
Here it was noticed that rupture of the steel was seen at the tension plane,
whereas plastic deformation was seen at the shear plane, but no rupture.
This was concluded to indicate that an incorporation of the ultimate strength
when tension is considered and the yield strength when shear is considered.
Franchuk et al. [8] made tests on 17 coped beam specimens with diп¬Ђerent
bolt layouts. The general outcome from the tests showed a slightly under-
estimation of the capacity when considering both Canadian and American
standards. However, for some of the specimens an overestimation was on
the other hand seen, making the standards non conservative. Summing up,
these results show a clear inconsistency of the design equations regarding
block tearing.
The tests was continued until a total rupture of the connection was seen,
yielding a very ductile behaviour with large deformations of the individual
tests. It was not stated whether tension rapture happened prior to shear
rupture or vice versa. In the tests, conп¬Ѓguration of specimens with the same
net tension- and shear area but diп¬Ђerent gross shear area was made. Here
results showed higher capacity of the conп¬Ѓgurations with higher gross area,
supporting the theory that gross area should be used in the determination
of bearing capacity concerning shear. The eп¬Ђect of an eccentric load was not
considered in these tests.
A conclusion was made on the fact that end distance and bolt spacing
aп¬Ђects coped beams in the same manner as they do to gusset plates. They
further conclude on beam end rotation, and states that this has no eп¬Ђect on
the block shear capacity.

2.1 Literature study
Literature study
Franchuk et al.
[9] further examines the block tearing capacity of coped
beams as outlined in the design standards in Canada, America, Europe and
Japan. Here the conclusion is given that for the connections examined in the
paper, few provide a reasonable capacity, however, often highly overestimated
and non conservative design capacities are as well seen.
The suggested design equations does not take the bolt layout, considering
end- and edge distances, nor the number of bolt lines into account, other than
the directly associated changes in shear- and tension areas.
Not only a non conservative design strength is seen, also the equations
suggested are inconsistent. From the tests performed, great variations in the
capacity between connections with one and two bolt lines was seen and the
capacity predicted where grossly non conservative for two-line connections.
Figure 2.2: Mean test-to-predicted ratios for design equations. Table 3 in
[9].
A conclusion states that only the shear- and tension area needs to be
considered, when designing for block shear capacity, as can be seen in the
majority of the design equations governed by the standards.
A Finite Element parametric study was made by Topkaya [23] in 2004. By
this study more than one thousand simulations was conducted, of which 198
considered in-plane eccentricity. The amount of eccentricity was stated as
20-40% of the depth of the member, but the member depth was not stated.
As well as the eccentricity, the length of the connection was varying. A con-
clusion was made that for some connections, presence of eccentricity reduces
the block tearing strength, but the capacity was mostly reduced by the longer
connection. A proposal for the block shear capacity was suggested as:
Rn =

of the leading bolt to the end of the plate. The proposed design equation
does not cover eccentricity, but a conclusion is made that a reduction of 10%
should be considered, when designing a connection subjected to eccentricity.
Driver et al. [7] compared the test-to-predicted ratio of a database hold-
ing 205 block shear tests from 17 diп¬Ђerent research programs, see the table
in Figure 2.4. By the use of this database and the failure modes observed
during testing, an uniп¬Ѓed design equation is as well proposed, governing all
diп¬Ђerent elements subjected to block shear. The proposed design equation is
given as (Eq. (15) in the table on Figure 2.4):

lines.
Figure 2.3: Stress factors for uniп¬Ѓed equation. Table 5 in [7].
By the proposed design equation gross area is used and hereby states that
the shear failure happens on the outside of the bolt-line. Consideration on
the strength development regarding shear is as well made. It is described that
a signiп¬Ѓcantly higher eп¬Ђective shear strength is seen, based on the results of
numerous research programs. In the design proposal an average of the shear
yield and ultimate strength is taken as the eп¬Ђective shear strength.
The structure of the design equation is further kept simple, so that a
translation between the diп¬Ђerent types of structural elements subjected to
block tearing is straightforward.

2.1 Literature study
Literature study
Figure 2.4: Mean test-to-predicted ratios for research program. Table 1 in
[7].
Regarding rotational block tearing J. JВЁonsson proposed in one article [15] the
capacity of both C- and L-block tearing of gusset plates. The proposal for the
design strength was presented at the Nordic Steel Construction Conference
2015 in Tampere, Finland. His proposal for block tearing capacities includes
a distribution of shear and normal forces applied from the moment yielded
by an eccentric applied force. However, some students had prior performed
experiments on the C-block tearing, that showed a fairly conservative guess of
the capacity, by this model. This master thesis orients on this proposal, why
a more throughout description on this is given further down in the thesis.
2.1.1
Summary
Over time, several specimens have been tested, including both angles, coped
beams, and gusset plates. Only for a few, the eп¬Ђect of the load being eccentric
has been subject to testing, hence for most of the specimens, this has not
been the case.
In the tests where angles where subjected to an eccentricity, the actual
eccentricity was minimal. However, a conclusion was made that the eccen-
tricity might have great inп¬‚uence on the shear capacity, but for the tension
capacity no further inп¬‚uence was concluded. The opposite was stated by
10
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:25==
Literature study
2.1 Literature study
the factors in Figure 2.3, where only the tension area seems aп¬Ђected by the
in-plane eccentricity. As well no conclusion was made on the fact that the
eccentricity introduces a moment.
More than once it is stated that the gross area can be used when shear
load is considered, while net area should be used when tension is the govern-
ing force. For the tension strength the Whitmore eп¬Ђective width is as well
mentioned, where a larger eп¬Ђective area can be considered. Regarding shear
strength, some considered the correlation between connection length and the
eп¬Ђective strength. Driver et al. [7] considered a mean strength as the eп¬Ђec-
tive shear strength. However, for most of the cases yield strength is seen to
be the strength regarding shear, whereas ultimate strength is utilised when
tension is the primary force. The reasoning for this is given by the fact that
rupture was often seen in the tension part, where large plastic deformation
yielded in the parts subjected to shear.
An overall conclusion for the diп¬Ђerent tests, was made on the fact that great
inconsistency in the design standards is seen. The majority of the design
standards used in the analysis only uses the shear- and tension area as the
design parameter, which is also the conclusion from Franchuk et al. [9]. How-
ever, Franchuk et al. makes conп¬‚icting conclusions as a statement is made
on both the bolt layout being neglectful and at the same time stating that
connections with two bolt lines yield highly non conservative capacities.
For the diп¬Ђerent proposals for a new design standard, connection lengths
is taken into consideration. Also, even though it is almost neglected, the dif-
ferent design proposals includes some capacity reduction regarding eccentric
load. This is seen in equation (2.3) governed by the shear line eccentricity
es, by equation (2.4) with the suggestion of a 10% reduction and by equation
(2.5) included in the stress factors. However, none of the proposals takes the
eccentricity into direct consideration.

Chapter 3
Theory
In the chapter holding theory, four main topics are investigated. The topics
of concern are the design by Eurocode, where the requirements given by the
standard in Europe is inspected, including a suggestion on a new formulae
governing block tearing, for the future Eurocode. Next is how forces are
distributed in the connection investigated. This section examines both the
force distribution from the bolts to the plate, but mainly how the force is
distributed in the block of the plate that is exposed to tearing. Afterwards
yield criteria are inferred and a suggestion of how to include more capacity
is given. Finally an introduction of the block tearing component into the
Component method is given. By the introduction of this component it is
sought to determine the behaviour of the block, when subjected to normal-
and shear forces, however especially moment is of great concern.
3.1
Design by Eurocode
In order to secure the design capacity of a bolted connection, capacity of
all individual parts has to be veriп¬Ѓed. In the following subsections, design
calculations for the individual parts are stated, as given by the Eurocode [5].
However, in the further work in the thesis, focus is on block tearing and the
capacity of the remaining components are hereby assumed veriп¬Ѓed compared
to the block tearing.
The connection considered in this thesis is a Category A - Bearing type.
13
==PAGE:28==

3.1.4
Block tearing
Block tearing according to Eurocode is described as a failure in shear along
the shear face of the bolt group and at the same time tensile rupture along
the line of bolts on the tension face. The Eurocode suggests both a design
equation for a bolt group subjected to a con- and eccentric applied load. For
a concentric load, the resistance Veff,1,Rd is given as
Veff,1,Rd = fu

factor for resistance of cross-sections and ОіM2 is partial factor for resistance
of cross-sections in tension to fracture.

As one notice, the only diп¬Ђerence is the fact that for the eccentric loaded bolt

how big of an eccentricity the formula can be applied. For both the concentric
and eccentric load, shear capacity is by Eurocode standard governed by the
net area.
New proposal
However, by the continuing development and research in the engineering п¬Ѓeld,
the Eurocode undergoes norm work and new versions of the design standard
arrives from time to time. In 2020 a new proposal for the next generation
design standard is under review, including a new proposal for the design of
block tearing capacity [1]. One noticeable diп¬Ђerence is the fact that the п¬Ѓrst
paragraph states that (1) Block tearing should be avoided. This statement
might be a consequence of the great insecurity that is seen, when dealing
with block tearing, however at the same time requiring the need of the ca-
pacity formula to be correct. One simply cannot avoid block tearing if a false
assurance is given by a wrong formula.
The new proposal for concentric block tearing capacity is given by

3.2 Force distribution
3.2
Force distribution
In order to determine the bearing capacity of the block, it is important to
investigate how the force is distributed in the diп¬Ђerent parts of the connection.
The transfer of forces from one element to another is through the bolts. It
is therefor important to be aware of how forces are distributed via the bolts.
3.2.1
Force distribution based on Eurocode 3
For the eccentric loaded connection, force is distributed diп¬Ђerently depending
on which force is applied. For the normal- and shear force, load is distributed
evenly among the bolts, in the respectively directions, see Figure 3.1. This
yield that for the normal force, a horizontal component of

is given, while a vertical component from the shear force is given as

3.2 Force distribution
Theory
Elastic distribution
In the theory of elasticity, force is distributed in a way, such that the element
furthest away from the center of rotation is subjected to the greatest force.
A description on this is given in [10]. The center of rotation is found based
on equilibrium by placing the two main axis x and y in the centroid of the
bolt group, with the y-axis parallel to the force applied, see Figure 3.2.

3.2 Force distribution
3.2.2
Generalized block failure
In the conference paper from the Nordic Steel Construction Conference in
2015 [15] a distribution of the individual normal force N, shear force V and
moment M are suggested, for both a C- and L-cutout. The distribution of
the individual forces are perfectly plastic permissible distributions based on
horizontal-, vertical- and rotational equilibrium respectively. The eccentric-
ity of the force in the connection is afterwards made by rotational equilibrium
around any given point.
Some assumptions are made in the proposed generalized block tearing method.
At п¬Ѓrst a mean of the yield- and ultimate strength is used as the yielding
criterion.
This assumption is made on behalf of the fact that the strain
hardening of the yield lines vary over the length, hence strain hardening
will commence before the yield mechanism has fully developed. The mean
strength fm is given by:
fm = fy + fu
2
(3.24)
Another important assumption is that normal force is assumed to act over
the net section area of the yield lines, whereas shear force is assumed to act
upon the total gross section area of the yield lines. It is further assumed that
the moment acts as a normal force in yield lines parallel to the applied force
and as a shear force in the yield lines perpendicular to the applied force.
The yield criterion proposed in the paper is given by the simple form of
von Mises, hence
Пѓ2 + 3П„ 2 в‰¤f 2
m
(3.25)
As one might notice on Figure 3.6 and 3.7, the yield lines subjected to shear
is in advance reduced by 1/
в€љ
3. This is done to simplify the capacity check,
which is described later.
To describe the resistance against normal-, shear- and moment forces, the
maximum dissipated distributions of the respective forces are given below,
for the C- and L-cutout respectively. The respective resistance is indexed
with subscript R.

3.2.3
Other plastic distributions
The generalized block failure is one of many solutions to the failure problem.
By the theory of plasticity, as was used in in the previous section, several
diп¬Ђerent force distributions are allowed, as long as equilibrium is at place.
Finding an equilibrium that yields a lower resistance is always possible. How-
ever, to infer a solution where equilibrium is obtained and at the same time
exceeding resistance of all of the other solutions, that is the diп¬ѓcult part.
In the following, diп¬Ђerent other plastic distributions are investigated, to
see if this could yield a higher resistance.
To ease the understanding of
utilised geometric values and reference to the yield lines, Figure 3.8 is given.
hg
bg
hn
bn
Line 1
Line 2
t
Figure 3.8: Geometry of the block and naming of yield lines.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
25
==PAGE:40==
3.2 Force distribution
Theory
For most of the new suggestions of how force is distributed, stress from
normal- and shear force is distributed in the same way, as seen for the gener-
alized block failure in Figure 3.7. For these distributions agreement is made
with most standards and other suggestions found in the literature study,
that normal force act through the net area, while shear force acts through
the gross area. Only here a mean strength is utilised, due to the assumption
of varying strain hardening, whereas in the standards, division between yield-
and ultimate strength is normally seen.
The diп¬Ђerence in the suggested distributions, is given by how the moment
from the eccentric applied force is distributed.
For all of the distributions, assumption about mean strength fm is still used.

wise displacement of the block would only be vertical. The only horizontal
displacement would be at the bottom, which is seen to be into the plate itself.

might yield the capacity, depending on the forces acting on the connection.
For these distributions, more than one interaction formulae is boxed. How-

the governing interaction for distribution B and D respectively.
For distribution E a higher moment capacity was found, when compared
to the generalized block failure in [15]. The moment yields both normal-
and shear stresses in both yield lines, why a distribution of the moment is
included in the interaction formula.
Further E/2 yield only one interaction formula, though Figure 3.13 shows
two. This is true because of the distribution factor q1 which, when below one,
introduces only compression in yield line 2, hence a division of the yield line
is not necessary.
3.2.5
Inп¬‚uence of friction
The connection of concern in this thesis is a category A, bearing type con-
nection. For this type of connection, friction is normally not included as a
bearing property, however, the inп¬‚uence of friction could yield greater resem-
blance between the capacity calculations and test results.
Every bolted connection is preloaded in some way, where preload is the ten-
sion created in the bolt, when the bolt is tightened. This preload function to
prevent opening of the connected parts and in some cases to prevent slippage
as well through friction.

3.3 Yield criteria
3.3
Yield criteria
At small stress levels steel behaves as a linear elastic material, where small
deformations are seen. This behaviour cease when the stress reaches a speciп¬Ѓc
level. From here the steel shows large deformations at relatively small stress
increments. At this point, steel is said to yield, hence the naming yield point
deп¬Ѓned by Greek letter Пѓy. The yield point is deп¬Ѓned as the point in uniaxial
tension of the steel, where yield, and corresponding large strain, is happening.
Оµ
Пѓ
Пѓy
Linear-elastic Perfectly-plastic
Figure 3.16: Stress-strain curve from uniaxial tension test, including curve
of a Linear-elastic Perfectly-plastic material. Principle sketch.
It is by experiments shown that at any arbitrary state of stress, the com-
bination of principal- and deviatoric stress, determine the yield point, and
not only the principal stress as one could think of. This combination of stress
is a so-called Yield criterion.
Yield criterion described by von Mises is the most common, regarding
steel structures.
3.3.1

The von MisesвЂ™ yield criterion base on the hypothesis that the size of the de-
viatoric stresses determines if the material yields. To reach this assumption,
the stress tensor Пѓ, written as

3.4 Component method
Theory
3.4
Component method
In the component method, a connection is described through the mechanical
behaviour of the individual components of the connection. The components,
are the parts which are contributing to the structural properties of the con-
nection. In this case, concern is only made on the component describing the
block tearing.
When talking component behaviour, stiп¬Ђness is the center of attention.
Through stiп¬Ђness the deformation of the connection is able to be described
from the loads applied.
In the Eurocode 1993-1-8 [5] the component method is introduced, how-
ever only the relation between moment and angular displacements are con-
sidered. In the following it is sought to expand the theory by inclusion of
moment in the plane of a gusset plate, hereby giving the block tearing stiп¬Ђ-
ness.
With use of the component method, location of the rotational center and
general behaviour of the connection is sought to be predicted.
3.4.1
Elastic springs
In [5] the behaviour of the components is described through elastic springs,
which can be connected either in serial- or in parallel connections.
The
behaviour of an elastic spring, follows the theory described by HookeвЂ™s law,
in which a force F and deformation Оґ are related via a spring constant k,
hence
F = k Оґ
(3.125)
In steel, a direct translation can be made considering the stiп¬Ђness parameter
given by YoungвЂ™s modulus of elasticity E which by the deformation described
by the strain Оµ, yields a stress Пѓ, hence
Пѓ = E Оµ
(3.126)
As well as deformation caused by normal stress, illustrated by Пѓ, deformation
caused by shear stress П„ can be described
П„ = G Оі
(3.127)
, where G is the shear modulus given by
G =
E
2(1 + ОЅ)
(3.128)
and Оі the shear strain. ОЅ is another material characteristic, describing the
strain interaction between diп¬Ђerent directions. For steel ОЅ = 0.3.

Chapter 4
Experiments
4.1
Introduction
Alongside the period of writing this thesis, experiments have been performed
on eccentric loaded bolted connections, of which the test setup is illustrated
on Figure 4.1. The experiments have been performed by fellow master stu-
dents for their individual master thesisвЂ™. The students performing the exper-
iments was:
вЂў Type A: C-cutout, two 5 mm external plates, by Lasse T. MГёlsted [18]
вЂў Type B: L-cutout, one 10 mm internal plate, Andreas E. A. Nielsen
[19]
вЂў Type C: L-cutout, two 5 mm external plates, by Kamil E. Czerniak [3]
The setup is designed so that capacity of the rotational block tearing can
be investigated for an applied point load with varying eccentricities.
On
the setup diп¬Ђerent measuring devices was placed, whereby measures such as
rotation, vertical displacement and strain could be logged.
Figure 4.1: Test setup in 3D. Figure from [24].
67
==PAGE:82==
4.2 Test setup
Experiments
4.2
Test setup
In 2014 Steen Wintherskov-Andersen [24], for his Master thesis, performed a
sensitivity analysis on the test setup, securing that failure of the setup would
happen as a rotational block tearing in the test plate. The sensitivity analy-
sis was performed for a C-block failure, but can be used for the L-cutout as
well, as capacity for this type of failure is beneath failure by C-block tearing.
The European standard DS/EN 1090-2:2018 [4] on execution of steel- and alu-
minium structures, with focus on the technical requirements for steel struc-
tures, sets up some rules and requirements which is sought to be respected in
the tests. Requirements regarding cutting, boltholes, bolts, nuts and washers
are all met. In the tests washers was used under the bolt head as well as
under the nuts.
For non-preloaded connections, as of category A connections, it is required
to secure a snug-tight condition, with special care of not to over-tightening
the bolts [5]. The over-tightening requirement especially goes for short- and
M12 bolts, as used in the tests. Every M12 bolt was tensioned using a torque
wrench to an even value of 57 Nm.

4.3 Test results

4.2.1
Tension test
Tension tests was performed to determine the strength and stiп¬Ђness proper-
ties of the plates used in the tests. Each of the test plates was delivered with
two dog-bones attached, one in each of its main directions, see Figure 4.4.
Tension tests was performed by the writer of this thesis.
The tests was performed as prescribed in the European standard DS/EN
ISO 6892-1 [6], with requirements of geometry, testing rates and data log-
ging being fulп¬Ѓlled. A more detailed description is given in [21].
Figure 4.4: Test plates was delivered with dog-bones attached.
The test yielded several material properties, however, for the sake of this
thesis, only yield- and ultimate strength, fy and fu, as well as stiп¬Ђness E is
directly used. The results from the testing can be seen in section 4.3.4.
4.3
Test results
In the following subsections, a summary of results from the three block tear-
ing tests and the tension tests are given. Moreover п¬Ѓgures showing the frac-
ture of some of the tests are shown, including comments on the behaviour
seen.
A comment on the strength: Yield strength of the block is found by the
intersection of a line yielding the stiп¬Ђness of the connection K and a line
70
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:85==
Experiments
4.3 Test results
given by K/10 which act as a tangent to the force-displacement curve, see
example on Figure 4.5. Ultimate strength is simply found as the maximum
applied force.
Figure 4.5: Example of how to п¬Ѓnd yield- and ultimate strength. Example
given by plate B4-3.
A comment on the rotation given in the tables in the following sections:
On the setup two rotational measuring devices was attached, one on the setup
itself (О±A) and one on the testing plate (О±B). As the devices are attached
on the opposite side respectively to each other, addition of the individual
measures yields total rotation of the connection, henceforth called О±.
In
the respective test reports, rotation of the tests is found in several ways
including vertical piston movement, distance gauges and strain measures,
however, these are all made on assumptions, why the rotational measuring
devises is assumed most accurate.
The rotation at yield is for Type A and C given by the rotation of the
point on graph which corresponds to the yield strength. For Type B rota-
tional measure at yield is given as the rotation of the actual yield strength,
meaning the rotation at K-K/10 intersection point.
A comment on center of rotation (RC) on п¬Ѓgures showing plates after test-
ing in the following sections: The lines on the п¬Ѓgures represents RC. During
testing lines where drawn along the edge of the plate of the setup, hereby
yielding the axis in both vertical and horizontal direction of which the con-
nection rotates about. Lines are afterwards drawn perpendicular through the
point of which the lines drawn during testing overlap.

By Figure 4.10 bolts from test C4-5+6 is depicted, but shows a typical
outcome for the Type C tests. From Figure 4.9 it is noticed that, as in the
Type B test, the upper and lower bolt in the vertical yield line, is subjected
to the greatest force. However, fracture is seen in bolt 6d, the lower bolt in
the yield line, of which the distance to RC seen by Figure 4.9d appears to be
much closer than the upper bolt.
This fracture is explained by the fact that in the upper corner, the plate is
pulled apart, yielding more of the stress going into the plate. In lower corner,
the plate is compressed against the bolt, yielding shear in bolt. Other than
that, the shear plane is going through the thread of the bolt, yielding a lower
capacity regarding shear resistance of the bolt.
No further deformation is noticed for the remaining bolts.

4.3.5
Discussion of experimental results
The Type A connection shows, as expected, higher resistance to the eccentric
applied force and the deformation yielded block tearing as expected too.
By comparison of Type B and C it is noticed that when comparing yield
strength, Type C yields higher resistance, but when comparing ultimate
strength the opposite is seen. The strength values are nevertheless some-
what close to each other. This comparison is showed in Table 4.8 where a
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
81
==PAGE:96==
4.3 Test results
Experiments
comparison between rotation angles as well are given.
By comparing the rotation angles, it is noticed that the thicker Type B
plates yields more rotation than the two thinner Type C plates. This obser-
vation might be explained by the fact that the thicker plates showed a higher
ductility in the tensile tests, where longer percentage elongations was seen.
Further it can be explained by the fact that the introduced strains Оµz in the
direction perpendicular to the faces of the plate, shows a linear relation to the
decrease in circumference, but a non-linear convex relation to the reduction
in cross-sectional area. Hereby the area reduction until ultimate capacity is
reached happens in the two thinner plates, before the thicker alone.

That the angle for the individual type rotated to a somewhat similar
extend, yields the assumption that the material can only deform so much,
before ultimate strength is reached.
The vertical distance between RC and the nearest horizontal edge y yields
somewhat similar average for Type B and C, where the average is given as
102.6 and 100.3 respectively.
When studying the yield lines of Type B and C it is for the vertical yield line
clear to see that the normal force from the action of the moment controls
the path. Especially by Figure 4.9a, a clear fracture of the net area is seen.
Necking of the plates is noticed in the areas of fracture, with uni-axial be-
haviour as seen on Figure 3.26. Shear deformation is more diп¬ѓcult to detect,
as the path follows the direction of the acting force, hence shear deformation
yields path along every elongated bolt hole.
For the horizontal yield line, compression is seen near the edge and tension
near the corner bolt. A slightly curved yield line is observed by studying the
grid just below row of bolt holes, giving the presumption that shear follows
the gross area, but deviate from this, by the impact of normal stresses from
the eccentric force applied.

4.4 Test compared to theory
4.4
Test compared to theory
In this section test results corresponding to the L-cutout is treated by being
put up against theoretical values found by the diп¬Ђerent force distributions
in section 3.2. In more general is the Generalized Block Failure (GBF) put
up against distribution A, B and E to п¬Ѓnd the distribution governing the
highest resistance. Further a comparison to the capacity found by use of the
present and suggestion to future Eurocode made.
Placement of the rotation center is as well compared to the theory. A
comparison between elastic center and center found from the use of Compo-
nent method is made.
In the setup, no acting normal force is applied, hence N = 0. The load
applied acts as a shear force with an eccentricity related to the shear force
center, hence ev-values given in Table 4.1. With this eccentricity a moment
arises, which is deп¬Ѓned by M = V ev. This rewriting of moment, is used in
the further analysis.
Initially the conditions in (3.66) and (3.67) is checked, to see if Whitmore-
or GBF distribution should be used to describe shear capacity:

4.4 Test compared to theory
Experiments
a conclusion can be made. The governing yield conditions are given by the
boxed interaction formulae in section 3.2.4. By inserting the relations N = 0

Friction contribution
As already stated in section 3.2.5, friction does not apply for a category A
connection. However, to investigate if the contribution from friction would
yield better coincide with the experimental values, a theoretical contribution
is calculated.
The calculation of the contribution can be seen in Appendix H.
In this
calculation, values in Table 4.16 are used.
Table 4.16: Values used in the friction calculation
Mv,app

By these values the clamping force in each bolt is calculated to
Fclamp = 26.9 kN
And the total resistance against friction to
FR,fric = 19.4 kN
The friction however does not lead a direct contribution to the yield crite-
rion, as the force introduces stress in a direction perpendicular to the plane
stresses, that is assumed in the yield criterion. Instead this friction acts as
an extra contribution to the capacity expression given by (4.5), so that a
total capacity including friction would seem:
FR,tot = FR,E + FR,fric
(4.6)
By a comparison with the experimental values at yield, friction seems to gov-
ern a resistance between 14-21% of the capacity. However, for more than one
of the experiments initial slip was observed. By the relatively high fraction of
the capacity, and the fact that initial slip was observed the presumption that
friction does not contribute to the capacity of this particular connection, is
sustained.
From here friction in the connection is neglected.

Chapter 5
Theory development
In this chapter development of the capacity calculations for the eccentric
loaded gusset plate connection is made. This is done based on the п¬Ѓndings in
Chapter 2 Literature study, Chapter 3 Theory and Chapter 4 Experiments.
5.1
Theoretical development
In the comparison between actual and theoretical values in section 4.4, it
was concluded that distribution E yielded the highest capacity. Therefore
distribution E is of main concern in the development of a new expression for
the capacity.
In the review of the various chapters, some observations have been made.
Below three diп¬Ђerent elements to be incorporated are given:
вЂў (1) It was noticed that in the compressed zones of the block, compres-
sion was going through the shear plane of the bolts, hence it is assumed
that gross area can be used in the zones regarding compression.
вЂў (2) Fracture happens as a necking of the upper tensional part of the
block. By this observation, an assumption of using ultimate strength
in the tension- and compression zone is made.
вЂў (3) Yield criteria was investigated, showing an actual increase of ca-
pacity, when п¬‚ow rule is included. This is based on uni-axial strain, on
which an observation was made.
These elements are incorporated in the following subsections.
91
==PAGE:106==
5.1 Theoretical development
Theory development
5.1.1
Bullet (1) Compression zone
Both by the experimental observations, but also allowed by the Eurocode,
compression is assumed to be going through the bolts, hence the gross area in
the compression zones can be used. The inclusion of this assumption yields
a new equilibrium for the distribution. Regarding normal- and shear force
resistance, NR and VR respectively, this assumption yields no diп¬Ђerence, as
no compression occurs. Hence, only equilibrium regarding moment is chang-
ing.

satisп¬Ѓed. For the distribution to the left on Figure 3.13, no diп¬Ђerence is seen
for q2 < 1 when compared to (3.53), as only a tension and shear zone is at
place. Hereby this capacity is written as

5.2
Comparison after theoretical development
In this section, a comparison is made between the experimental results and
the developed capacities. The comparison is divided so that both bullet (1)
and (2) is compared with the experimental results, both with and without
inclusion of bullet (3). In the п¬Ѓrst table showed below, shear and moment
resistances are shown for bullet (1) and (2) for each of the coupled strength
values respectively. When calculating the capacities, it was noticed that q1-
factor regarding both (1) and (2) was above 1, in contrast to the distribution
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
97
==PAGE:112==
5.2 Comparison after theoretical development
Theory development
before implementation of bullets.
If a comparison is made with Table 4.9, the improved capacities in Table 5.1
always yields higher resistance. The presumption that compression through
the gross area would yield a higher resistance, is seen when comparing the
moment capacity from the generalized block failure with the bullet (1) MR,1-
capacities. Here an increase of approximately 1.4% is seen, yielding a higher,
but relatively small increment in capacity.
The resistance to shear VR in both bullet (1) and (2) is relatively high com-
pared to the capacity found by the Eurocode. One can not directly compare
with the values stated in Table 4.13 regarding Eurocode capacities, as these
values are for an eccentric load, while the shear resistance is found from a
concentric acting load. However, if a comparison is made with (3.8) and
(3.10), the values stated in Table 5.1 still yields a higher resistance. The dis-
tribution of force is more or less the same, however, the assumption of using
the mean strength, leads to the high resistance. The fact that a rotation
acts at the same time as the shear stress, makes it a fair assumption that the
varying strain over the length of the yield line, leads to commence of strain
hardening, before the yield mechanism has been fully developed. Hereby a
greater strength is seen.

Table 5.2, 5.3 and 5.4 shows the capacity from bullet (1) and bullet (2)
compared with the experimental data. This comparison is made both by
the inclusion of п¬‚ow rule, but a comparison without this inclusion is as well

п¬‚ow rule in bullet (3), the average ratio is given to 96.3%. Both ratios yields
an immediate conservative suggestion of the capacity.

inclusion of п¬‚ow-rule, an increase in the average ratio is seen to about 102.4%
and with the inclusion of п¬‚ow-rule 108.7%, both making the suggested ca-
pacity just non-conservative.
A correlation between experimental and calculated values is clearly seen
by the fact that the ratios coincide with the average of the individual capacity.
Hereby the deviation of the results is small.

The assumptions regarding value x and y in (3.174) and (3.173) respec-
tively has changed, due to the new distribution. By the new distribution,
both horizontal and vertical equilibrium is made, hence both a value x cor-
responding to the compression zone in the horizontal yield line, and value y
corresponding to the tension zone in the vertical yield line, are found. These
values should of course not be confused with x and y in the Tables, which is
the calculated distances to RC from the edge and end of the block.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
105
==PAGE:120==
5.2 Comparison after theoretical development
Theory development
By the inclusion of these assumptions, a more accurate prediction of RC
is seen, when compared to the prior assumptions in section 4.4. By distance
y, the component method yield values fairly similar to the average of the
actual rotation center. The average is compared with, as Type B and Type
C shows diп¬Ђerent tendencies in the variation of distance y.
Regarding distance x still a fairly large deviation to the actual RC is
seen. However, the calculated value yield far more accurate results than for
the prior assumptions of starting value x.
The rotation angles calculated by the new assumptions yield a relatively
small increase in rotation. However, the deformation of the block, given by
(3.170) and (3.171) respectively, yields more accurate presumptions about
the actual behaviour. This is noticed by uy(0) в‰ѓ3.5 mm and uy(bg) в‰ѓв€’2.1
mm concluding on the behaviour seen by Figure 4.14. The horizontal defor-
mation ux(0) в‰ѓ4.8 mm and ux(hg) в‰ѓв€’2.9 mm, concludes the behaviour as
well, however, for the bottom part of the block, the inwards deformation is
not as clear, given the resistance from the adjacent material. The calculated
values can be found in Appendix E, F and G.
The use of the component method is based on many assumptions.
First
of all the component method is described through the theory of elasticity,
while the values used as input to x and y is given by the theory of plastic-
ity. An assumption is as well made on the stiп¬Ђness of the steel, given the
tri-linear stress-strain curve seen on Figure 3.37. Hereby an assumption of
the normally non-linear strain-hardening is included. The width of the yield
line by is as well given by an assumption. This value was found by former
master student Amanda Ibfelt [11], where she tried to implement the block
tearing component as well. All of these assumptions bring into question the
reliability of the use of the component method in relation to п¬Ѓnd the actual
behaviour of the block.

==PAGE:123==
Chapter 6
Conclusion
A literature study investigated some of the current knowledge on the topic
of rotational block tearing. It was here found that over time a great number
of plates was tested regarding block tearing, however, almost none of the
investigated plates, had taken the eп¬Ђect of en eccentric applied load, into
consideration. Several other aspects was investigated including bolt layout,
length of the connection, gross- and net-cross-section area and the use of
yield- or ultimate strength. Some concluded on the inп¬‚uence of eccentricity,
however, only a limited reduction of the capacity was suggested.
The theory regarding rotational block tearing was then investigated. Ini-
tially the design by Eurocode was studied, where it was found that the Eu-
rocode only include a simple reduction of 50% of the tension capacity, when
eccentricity is considered. It was further noticed that no limit was given on
this eccentricity, yielding that apparently boundless moment can aп¬Ђect the
connection without any further inп¬‚uence on capacity.
A suggestion of how force is distributed in the block, when subjected to
an eccentric force, was given by J. JВЁonsson [15] at a conference in 2015.
However, the capacity from this distribution was seen to yield lower capaci-
ties than actual experiments had shown. This distribution, that is based on
plastic equilibrium, was examined and several other diп¬Ђerent distributions
was proposed and further investigated. One distribution showed an actual
higher capacity, distribution E.
Next to be investigated was the yield criterion. The investigation took base
in the von Mises yield criterion, normally used in steel structures. It was
sought to improve the yield condition, by implementation of the п¬‚ow rule,
by looking at the strain behaviour. With the condition of uniaxial strain, an
109
==PAGE:124==
Conclusion
improvement of the von Mises yield criterion was possible, as a reduction on
the normal stress hereby was a condition.
Last in the theoretical chapter, a new component regarding rotational block
tearing was inferred, to be included in the component method. By this com-
ponent it was sought to predict the behaviour of the block when subjected to
normal force, shear force and moment. The component behaviour is based in
the theory of elasticity, but tries to predict behaviour after yield, why several
assumptions was put into the component. This included a tri-linear assump-
tion on the strain-hardening, an assumption on the width of the yield line
and п¬Ѓnally values from the plastic distributions was used in the derivation of
the component behaviour.
Experiments on rotational block tearing was performed by fellow students in
the same period as this thesis. Hereby data of the experiments was included
to make comparisons between theory and actual performance. It was noticed
that the investigated distributions yielded results showing the same tendency
as the experiments. However the resistance still showed fairly conservative
predictions. The deformation behaviour was as well compared, however, the
predicted behaviour only showed similar tendencies to the behaviour seen,
but an actual prediction is not found through the block tearing component.
With the theory and experiments examined, tendencies and observations
was put into the development of a new suggestion to a bearing capacity ex-
pression. The basis of the model was given on distribution E. Three main
elements was included: (1) Inclusion of gross-section area in compression
zones, (2) Inclusion of ultimate strength in tension and compression zones
and (3) Inclusion of new yield condition with п¬‚ow rule. An investigation of
the diп¬Ђerent implementations was performed, showing fairly great improve-
ment compared to the Generalized Block Failure and at the same time a
reasonable similarity almost coinciding with the experimental values. By the
development of distribution E, both stress distribution factors q1 & q2 se-
curing the eп¬Ђect of the height-to-width ratio, stress relation factors Оі1 & Оі2
securing the right relation between horizontal and vertical equilibrium and
strength factors ОІm & ОІu implemented to simplify the expressions, was intro-
duced. However, by the reviewing the experimental comparisons, strength
factors and inclusion of п¬‚ow rule is not implemented in the п¬Ѓnal suggestion
to a new capacity expression, given at the end of the thesis. This capacity
suggests about 90% of the actual strength with the admissible test results
taken into consideration. By the suggestion simplicity and reliability is the
priority, why the ОІ-factors and п¬‚ow rule is not included.
110
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:125==
Conclusion
6.1 Suggestions to future work
6.1
Suggestions to future work
The range of experimental data is quite limited, why more experiments
should be performed on the rotational block tearing. In this both several
diп¬Ђerent eccentricities and diп¬Ђerent bolt layouts should be target of investi-
gation.
A scaling factor of the capacities could be of interest to investigate further.
It is seen that every distribution yield similar tendencies to the actual ex-
perimental values, why a scaling of the simplest distribution might yield
appropriate and simple capacity expressions.
Tightening of bolts could be of interest to investigate further.
A limited
review on the friction contribution is given in this thesis, however, new as-
pects might come into mind with further investigation.
The prediction of deformation behaviour, regarding a block tearing com-
ponent, could be investigated further to support the prediction of how force
interacts in the block.
An upper bound solution to the problem could as well be of interest to
investigate.

Capacity calculations
In the first part of this appendix, the capacity is calculated on behalf of the expressions given i Chapter 3 - Theory. Here the Generalized
Block Tearing (GBT) is put up against distribution A, B and E, to find the governing distribution. 
In the second part is the capacity of the developt distribution calculated. 
For both parts, theory is compared to test results. 
In this document results for Type C with admissible testvalues is investigated.

Capacity calculations
In the first part of this appendix, the capacity is calculated on behalf of the expressions given i Chapter 3 - Theory. Here the Generalized
Block Tearing (GBT) is put up against distribution A, B and E, to find the governing distribution. 
In the second part is the capacity of the developt distribution calculated. 
For both parts, theory is compared to test results. 
In this document results for Type C with inadmissible testvalues is investigated.

As seen by the values xRCi all are found to a value greater than the width of the block bg. Therefore in this case, the model suggests
that the rotation center is placed outside the block, which is observed from experiments not to be correct.
On the next page, the behavior of the two yield lines are illustrated on the graphs. On the first graph, the horizontal yield line is shown.
From this graph it can be seen how deformation is greatest on the internal part of the block. It is further seen that with increasing
eccentricity, decreasing vertical deformation happens.
On the second graph deformation of the vertical yield line is seen. Here the method suggests the same center for all eccentricities and
at the same time a center near the actual center. Larger eccentricity yields larger deformations.

On the following page is the behavior of the yield lines illustrated on graphs. 
By the first graph horizontal yield line behavior is seen. Different from the previous assumption, the assumption of the starting point at
the same point as change in the compression and tension zone, yields far more correct results. However, the difference in actual and
found horizontal placement of rotation center is still somewhat large. 
It is noticed that the smaller eccentricity shifts the RC away form GC as in the theory of elasticity, and as seen by the experiment.
By the second graph behavior of the vertical yield line is seen. Here it is noticed that for alle the different eccentricities, rotation is
around the same point. The larger eccentricity is noticed to yield a higher amount of deformation.

rcx: The actual rotaion center is noticed to be very close to the elastic center. By the component method, only assumptions from the
developed theory yields somewhat correct results, however, still with a great difference to the actual values.
rcy: From the experiment an increase in rcy is seen with decreasing eccentricity. However this is not seen when the component
method is used, as the rotaion always is about the same point, when no normal force is acting. 
However, both GBT and Devel yield somewhat similar values that both coincide with the mean of the actual center.

As seen by the values xRCi all are found to a value greater than the width of the block bg. Therefore in this case, the model suggests
that the rotation center is placed outside the block, which is observed not to be correct.
On the next page, the behavior of the two yield lines are illustrated on the graphs. On the first graph, the horizontal yield line is shown.
From this graph it can be seen how deformation is greatest on the internal part of the block. It is further seen that with larger
eccentricity, smaller vertical deformation happens.
On the second graph deformation of the vertical yield line is seen. Here the method suggests the same center for all eccentricities and
at the same time a center nea the actual center. Larger eccentricity yields larger deformations.

On the following page is the behavior of the yield lines illustrated on graphs. 
By the first graph horizontal yield line behavior is seen. Different from the previous assumption, the assumption of the starting point at
the same point as change in the compression and tension zone, yields far more correct results. However, the difference in actual and
found horizontal placement of rotation center is still somewhat large. 
It is noticed that the smaller eccentricity shifts the RC away form GC as in the theory of elasticity, and as seen by the experiment.
By the second graph behavior of the vertical yield line is seen. Here it is noticed that for alle the different eccentricities, rotation is
around the same point. The larger eccentricity is noticed to yield a higher amount of deformation.

rcx: The actual rotaion center and elastic center does not coincide as for Type B. By the component method, only assumptions from
the developed theory yields somewhat correct results, however, still with a great difference to the actual values.
rcy: From the experiment a jump in rcy is seen not depended on eccentricity. However this is not seen when the component method is
used, as the rotaion always is about the same point, when no normal force is acting. 
However, both GBT and Devel yield somewhat similar values that both coincide with the mean of the actual center.

As seen by the values xRCi all are found to a value greater than the width of the block bg. Therefore in this case, the model suggests
that the rotation center is placed outside the block, which is observed not to be correct.
On the next page, the behavior of the two yield lines are illustrated on the graphs. On the first graph, the horizontal yield line is shown.
From this graph it can be seen how deformation is greatest on the internal part of the block. It is further seen that with larger
eccentricity, smaller vertical deformation happens.
On the second graph deformation of the vertical yield line is seen. Here the method suggests the same center for all eccentricities and
at the same time a center nea the actual center. Larger eccentricity yields larger deformations.

On the following page is the behavior of the yield lines illustrated on graphs. 
By the first graph horizontal yield line behavior is seen. Different from the previous assumption, the assumption of the starting point at
the same point as change in the compression and tension zone, yields far more correct results. However, the difference in actual and
found horizontal placement of rotation center is still somewhat large. 
It is noticed that the smaller eccentricity shifts the RC away form GC as in the theory of elasticity, and as seen by the experiment.
By the second graph behavior of the vertical yield line is seen. Here it is noticed that for alle the different eccentricities, rotation is
around the same point. The larger eccentricity is noticed to yield a higher amount of deformation.

rcx: The actual rotaion center and elastic center does not coincide as for Type B. By the component method, only assumptions from
the developed theory yields somewhat correct results, however, still with a great difference to the actual values.
rcy: From the experiment a jump in rcy is seen not depended on eccentricity. However this is not seen when the component method is
used, as the rotaion always is about the same point, when no normal force is acting. 
However, both GBT and Devel yield somewhat similar values that both coincide with the mean of the actual center.
